Edits because typos True Friends(They look nice, and they are nice. These are letters which look and sound just like English letters.): A, K, M, O, T
False Friends(They look nice(like the True Friends), but don't be fooled. These look like English letters you're familiar with, but sound different.): V, Je, N, R, S, U, H
New Friends(They might look scary, but you'll warm up to them easily. These are letters you haven't seen before, but they have similar sounds.): B, G, D, Z, I, L, P, F, E
Strangers(These will be the tricky ones. They look different from anything you've seen before(like the New Friends), and don't have similar sounds to English, either.): Jo, Å½, J, C, Ä†, Å , Å `, ', Ui, `, Ju, Ja If you've learned Hiragana and Katana, this really isnt intimidating. Tanks, also for this video. bol'shoye spaseeba As the native Russian speaker myself, I must correct you: that's a kind of meme now to say that in Russian they used to say "Yes no maybe" (Da nyet navyernoye). In fact though that da in the beginning plays the role of say it intensifier, emotional auxiliary senseless word... What "da nyet navernoye" translates to correctly LITERALLY would be like "Well, no, I think". The word da in Russian language has two meanings, at least. For example, look at da in the phrase "Da budyet svyet" - "Let there be light"... ĞŸÑ€Ğ¸Ğ²ĞµÑ‚,  ĞœĞ°Ñ€Ğ¸Ğ½Ğ°! Millions of black african would want to learn russian to live in Russia, for the beautyful white russian females. Good intro, but the hard sign explanation is a bit off. It does follow consonants, but it does not just "make them hard". For example, the L sound does not need the hard sign to be hard because it is actually already hard without it. Instead, the hard sound makes a vowel after it stumble as if there were 2 words there instead of one. For example, in English we'd say something like "we'd say", and this "we'd" piece sounds completely different from "wed", but although in English it's only used for binding 2 words together, in Russian it would be the same pronunciation effect, but weirdly within 1 word, and there would be no way to expand the word as it would be already complete and in its full version. When I was five, and had mastered the English alphabet, my father showed me the Cyrillic alphabet.   A few letters caught my fancy, such as the house with legs, the asterisk, the ten with a hyphen, and of course, the backwards R, but I did not know a word of Russian.   So, one day I got a felt pen, and started writing random Cyrillic letters on the garage wall,  Ğ¯, Ğ–, Ğ´ and Ñ.   My father saw my graffiti, and asked if I did that.  I hesitantly said I did, and thought I was in big trouble.  But he said, "Do you know what you just wrote?  You wrote 'I am waiting.'" 25 years ago I took two years of Russian in college. Russian, Bulgarian or Serbian (Cyrillic) alphabet is NOT based on Greek, as some major sources based on the history written by Greeks, would lead you to believe. The alphabet natively called AZBUKA (named after first two letters Az and Buki) is based on much older set of characters that originate in Vinca culture in the Balkans (approximately 5700-4500 BC). Greeks came to Balkans much later and mixed with native Proto-Serbian population and adopted the characters, at least the ones they could pronounce, as their speech apparatus was and still is different. Cyrill and Methodius did not spread the literacy among Slavs, as the Azbuka already existed. They were on the mission from Pope, to introduce Christianity to mainly Moravia in local alphabet. But they, especially Cyril, got the laurels for introducing literacy. And the mainstream history, even in Russia and Serbia, still supports this story. If you don't know someone in Russia who speaks the language, knows all about the bureaucracy (locations of gov. services, how and where to make appointments, costs), then you will have a difficult time. I recommend getting temporary residency permission. There's an initial language test you must take. You must have an Appostile done on your criminal record history or lack thereof. If you are in Russia at present, it will require an appointment to Moscow to get fingerprinted and a long waiting game after you mail your prints the U.S. That cost money too. You will be issued a new visa if you are successful, but you must not leave the country longer the 90 days in order to retain your visa. There are other avenues to get work permits using a work visa. Just be prepared to pay taxes regardless, and know the costs and processes. There are social taxes and medical insurance that must be paid too, which is calculated per month. If you do get temporary permission you must define yourself with the government, which will only allow you yo do certain types of work. You can be self employed or work for someone else. Your employer should pay your taxes, but the cost is so high many employers will avoid employing a foreigner. The whole Russian alphabet really is an unusual amalgamation! It's pretty easy to learn Russian Alphabets using pronunciation of Indian Alphabets (Devnagari Alphabets). I came so if you say zavut, why do you write zovut ??? Learning memorizing useful sentences  gives very poor results. Because students can't learn many. Every one can learn a few telephone numbers but learning a new telephon number every day is not sustainable. Same thing with sentences.
I think the way to learn languages is to learn to read  stories. Then students can gradually improve their reading skills with the help of the teacher. When they can read fluently and they can read abundantly ( free voluntary reading ,  reading the books they choose) then the brain acquires more and more vocabulary in a sustainable way. And students acquire the grammar too, even though they are not aware of it because it is done subconsciously by the brain. Listen this podcast ğŸ¤© just awesome ğŸ’¯ğŸ’¯â£ï¸ To everyone saying it is not for beginners I'd argue that it is, if you watch beginner listening practice on other languages you'll see it is of a similar difficulty. Russian is a very hard language, it takes a lot of effort and hard work to speak, but you can't lower the standard of how proficient a beginner should be because of this. Its normal that it takes far longer to understand listening practice for in beginners in Russian compared to Spanish for example. (If your native language is English) 90 day practice daily 3 hour Russian is one of those languages where you have to listen and speak and read as much as you possibly can, even if you make mistakes. The more you expose yourself to Russian like these videos, the more you will start to recognize frequently repeated words. Also if you learn words on your own, you will hear them in the things you watch, read, or listen to. It's a delicate balance of seeking out new words and letting your brain pick out words it wants to know from repeated encounters. It's slow-going but studying grammar and formal approaches won't be as effective as jumping in and just letting your spongey mind do its thing. This video doesn't mean you need to magically know all the words. But find the patterns like how in every segment it says "a man and woman are talking" or a similar phrase. After watching a few segments you now know how to say man, woman, talking, etc. Same with the questions and any words you find useful. What do YOU want to talk about? Learn those words and fill in the rest later. how is it for beginners?? So, right after the soviet union collapsed my family along with basically the entire village migrated from Kazakhstan back to Germany (the village consisted of german immigrants, who came to Russia under the rule of Catherine II. and were later relocated from the Wolga river far into the country by Stalin).
My family was part of it and they managed to start over here in Germany. What I now am really grateful for is that apart from being a native German speaker I can mostly understand my family speaking Russian as well, because I grew up with it more or less.
Now what IÂ´m trying to do is also learn the active part of Russian such as speaking and writing, since it always was left behind in a mostly german environment outside home.

Interestingly you can and should do the same in German. In the singular human is Mensch, in the plural it's Menschen, but there exists a second form, a plurale tantum that sounds very similar to the Russian equivalent and is likely related to it etymologically: Leute. Usually the correct plural of Mensch would be Leute, and the plural Menschen is reserved for referring to the species. But in the last decades people more and more resort to Menschen even though they mean Leute. Well, it might not be for absolute beginners, but if you listen it all the way through you will notice that it is a repeating pattern. You will almost all the time find the same people talking to each other (e. g. a man and a woman, a salesman/-woman and a woman etc.) and the vocabulary is not very diverse. As an absolute beginner this is ridiculously complicated, but if you are a little advanced and have learnt how to inflect nouns, adjectives and verbs, it should work out after some time of getting used to it. I found the Cyrillic keyboard quite easy, actually, when I posted a copy of the keyboard viewer handy to my screen. Then as I found the letter and typed it (without looking at the keyboard; I'm a touch-typer), I also sounded out the word. That way I became more familiar with both the typing and the sounds of the letters. Yes, it may be slower at the beginning, but the arrangement of the keys is so much easier to use that I soon caught up. Good Russian...Almost without an accent... Thanks a lot for kind words... It would be nice to watch russian movies with him and talk about them with him in russian Your pronunciation is already close to native, but here are a few tips on how you can improve even further:

- Ğ¨ and Ğ– are actually always hard instead of always soft, which is how you currently pronounce them. I'd say these two consonants are your biggest weakness right now.
- Similarly to the first point, Ñ‡ and, by extension, Ñ‰ are also always soft - which goes in stark contrast to Polish, so bear that in mind. Ğ§ should be pronunced similarly to Polish Ä‡, while to pronounce Ñ‰, you just merge Ñˆ and Ñ‡ together.
- As much as we love to reduce vowels and drop consonants, "Ñƒ" actually always stays a vowel, and is never pronounced as as semi-vowel ("w"), which is who you currently pronounce it.
